Project Descripdon
The Govenmen of Chad has developed an Educadon-Tralnng-Employment (ETh) strate  for
1990-2000: this project would support its implementation between 1994 and 1998.  ho ceta
poject objectve is to improve the access, quality and efficiency of primy educadon, primay in
rral areas and nationwide for girls.  To this end, the project would suppot a natl
development ptogrm for primary education designed to achieve the fWowing args   fr 1998:
rsing overall pimary enrollment ratio from 59% in 1990 to 65% and the shae of female
erlment from 31% to 36%, and reducing the average repetition ats f*om 30% to 20%.
Secondary objectves are to promote a more deicient resource use in the educaton sector.
To achieve these objectives and to help finance recurrent costs etaled by project Invomen, the
project would support policy meaes to: (a) increa public expdure on pimay ducation
(b) Increase efficiency in the use of primary school teachers and dasoms; (c) promot gis'
partiipation in primary education; and (d) encourage prive initiatives in th  prvision of
education.
Wihin the framework of these policy measures, the project would finance Iwesm   dened to:
1. In=rv thle acess and quallt of gdM=r educatin natSonwide b:
(a) construcn  800 primary school clssms nd rehabiating 200 claom;
(b) purchasing and distrbung about 780,000 primary textbooks and 10,000 teahr
guid;
(c) restucturing and improving preservice and inservice teacher training;
(d) strengthening sol inspections and teacher supervision;
(e) developing and implementing a program to accelerate girls' education.
2.    Support a pilot idmool improvement proam for adm     edumdo  In dre rions
(a) promoting locally-based pedagogic improvement projects In 90 Indvidua schools;
(b) tetng an impwved student assment program in 100 schools;
(C)           the management and organizaton capacity of 100 parn   t .



.iii-
3.    S h_ Cdllu l Setrl Institutos k.;
(a) fiurter developing the planning and management capacity of MEN;
(b) reinforcing MEN's capacity to prepare and implement donor-financed education
projects.
To mitigae the potential negative impact on project Implementio  of a weak central
adminisaon and an uncertain political and economic environment, the project has been designed
to draw heavily on the proven existing capacities of local organizaions, NGOs and the prvate
sector.
e d Benefis:   bnVIplementation of the overall education program supported by this project
would provide additional enrollment capacity for approximately 117,000 additional pupils from
1994 to 1998. The main direct impact of the project would be to provide classrooms for 55,000
pupils, largely in rural areas, where 84% of project financed classrooms would be constructed. It
is expected that the primary enrollment ratio in rural areas would increase from 51% to 59%,
whereas that of urban areas would improve from 91% to 95%. Since repetition is expected to
decline from 30% to 20%, intake rates into grade one of primay education would increase more
rapidly (from 64% in 1990 to 75% in 1998), and the number of school years invested for each
student who reaches grade 6 would decline from 18 to 9 by the end of the project. In addition,
project investments in textbooks, teacher training, girls' education as well as project support for
sectoral policies would improve education quality and efficiency throughout the sector. Project
qualitative inputs and policy measures to limit repetition would improve quaity throughout the
primary education system, thereby lowering the average number of years it takes to graduate. In
addin, pilot school improvement projects would further improve education quality and
strengthen local initiatives in three regions covering 36% of primary school enrollment in 1990.
Support for insttional development would improve management and sustainability of the
ucation sector, and budgetary policy measures would prevent distortions in resource allocations
withi, the education sector. in the longterm, the project would raise the productivity of the labor
force and contribute to increased well-being among the Chadian population.
Blak. The fagile political enironment and weak implementing capacity could jeopardize project
implementation. To minimie the effect of this risk, the project would promote the Government's
commitment to: (a) decentralize education management; (b) reinforce the operaional responsibility
and management of schools by regional directorates; (c) rely on the private sector, NGOs, and
communities to manage construction and school improvement proJects; and (d) furlher develop
stakeholder commitment through the continued use of participatory, objective-oriented project
planning and implementation methods (ZOPP). The support for local school improvement projects
is also designed to build on the resilience and executing capacity of local communities. Another
risk is dtat the Government may face difficulties in regularly paying teachers salaries. This risk
would be addressed directiy by the introduction of a special payment mechanism to facilitate
regular salary payment for primary school teachers consisting of improved salary payment
procedures and a teachers' guaranty fimd. In addition, this risk would be midgated by (a)
consensus-building with the Government under the ETE framework; (b) prioritzing this issue in
macroeconomic dialogue among the IW, IDA and the Government; (c) maing close
coordination with and support from the French and other bilateral agencies; (d) monitoring of
public expitues for primary educadon through the special payment mechanism and during
anual reviews of project implementation; and (e) maianing high levels of community financing.

REPUBLIC OF CHAD
BASIC EDUCATION PROJECT
(EDUATION 
I.    INTRODUCTION
1.1   The Govenment of Chad has requested IDA fincing for a project to implement part )f
its Educatlon-Tralnlng-Eployment (ETE) Strategy (pan. 2.33). The ceral objective of the
project is to enhance the access, quality and efficiency of primary education, primarily In rural
areas and for girls' access to primary education nationwide. Secondary objectves are to promote
more efficient resource use In the education sector. Total project cost   esstmd at US$33.8
million equivalent (net of taxes and duties), with a foreign exchange component of US$17.4
milion. The Government and communities would contribute-in kind or in cash-he equivalent of
US$8.8 million, KfW would finance US$3.4 million, and GTZ wold finmce US$2.2. The
remainig US$19.3 million wculd be fimanced by IDA.
1.2   The proposed project would be the fifth IDA-fmnancew opetion for the development of
education in Chad since 1968. Investment programs and policy measures supported through the
project are based on lessons learned from the first four projects, the ETE roundtable held in 1990
among the Government and the donors, sector studies commissioned between 1990 and 1992,
regional stakeholder meetins during and after the preparation mission, and a project preparadon
smin  conducted during the pre-appraisal mission. The project was processed by IDA in close
coordination with WFP, UNDP, FAC, UNICEF, EEC, GMZ, BMZ, NGOs, and represenatives of
the Assoiatons des Parents des ELAves (APE).
H.  SECORAL CONTEXT
2.1    Chad is one of the poorest countries in the world. Per Capita GNP is $210 (compared to
US$635 as an average for Sub-Saharan Africa (SSA)); life expectn   at birth is 47 years
(compared to 52 years); female adult literacy is 18% (38%); and total adult literacy is 30%
(49%)3. The population of 5.4 million inhabitants Is divided among about 110 ethic groups.
Sinc independence, recurring political instability and domestic strife bave thwarted development.
Agriculure-chiefly subsistence production of food crops by small holders-generates 35-40% of
GDP and provides a livelihood for more than 80% of the population. The industrial sector (20%
of GDP) includes a large cotton parastatal, public and para-public medium-scale enterprises, and
informal micro enterprises. The service sector (40-45% of GDP) includes a large informal trading
sector.
B. 13ono9mlDe  elDpmwl
2.2 A major economic adjustment has been launched since the end of the civil war (1979-83).
3Wodd Deveaopmt Inors, WorldDeveopnw Repm 1992; UASCO Satdcal Yearbook, 1992.



-2 -
Aftr the sever drought of 1984 and a subsequent drop In world coton prices, *he Govmuent-
suppotted by IDA and the DO-established an emergency program to reform the cotton sector. In
1987, this program was extended, under a comprehensive adjustment progrm, to nclude refoms
in tnsporn, livestock and trade. Progress was generally good: the financial viability of the
cotton company was grely improved, competitlon and private sector development improved In
transortaon and livestock. Progress to improve public resource management was mixed. Curet
penditures were partially restuctured to favor key development sectors and the share of
xpenditures on supplies and materials was increased. However, the civilian wage bill was
increased by 63% between 1988 and 1990: combined with an overrun on mlitay expenditures,
this caused the current fiscal deficit to increase from 2.2% of GDP in 1988 to 5.% in 1990.
During this period, public investment-which is totally externally fianced-rew from less than
5% of GDP in the early 1980s to nearly 10% in 1990.
2.3   Economic developments since the beginning of 1991 hWe been dominated by political
evns. The economy is currendy depressed, and enterprises in the formal sector are in danger of
collapse due to the unctain business climate linked in part to lower cotton earnings, withdrawal
of import protection because of difficulty enforcing customs procedures, and a build-up of public
sector payment arrears. The main budgetary problems have been serious overruns in military
expenditures and the civilian wage bill along with reduced revenue performance. The budget
deficit burgeoned in 1991 to about US$90.2 million (one and a half times larger than the 1990
deficit and roughly 7% of GDP against a target of 1.7%).
C. Pubhc and Commwdut-nx    dEdumtion
2.4   The forma education system in Chad is organized as In most other Sahelian counties. A
non-compulsory one-to-two year program of preschool education is followed by a six-year cycle of
primary educaion. Post-primary education includes a seven year cycle of secondary education-
fiur yeas of lower- and three years of upper-secondary schooling-and programs at the University
of Chad and at the Instt Supleu des Scences de l'Educadn in N'djamdna. In addition, the
education system includes teacher-taining colleges as well as technical and vocational schools.
2.5 By contrast, the level of development of community-based primary education in Chad is
unique in the Sahel. First, 400 local communities have created their own primary schools (6coks
spontanes) which are managed with little support from the Government. Of these co_mmnity
scools, 46% are incomplete and offer less than three grades: in total, they cover approximately
19% of primary enrollment. Second, even among government-recognized primary schools, local
APE assume major responsibility for financing school constuction and maintenance as well as
employing supplementary teachers. In addition, four types of specialized primary schools-
common to the Sahel-cover 2% of primary enrollment. Koranic schools, found in most villages,
only teach the Quoran; private Arabic schools teach religion and literacy; and small subsidized
catholic and private secuil schools teach the official curriculum.
2.6   In tota, approximately 2,150 public and private schools provide preschool, primary,
ndary, tecical and vocational training in Chad. Responsibility for all levels of fonnal
educadon lies with the Ministry of National Education (MEN); the Haw CoW de V'Educadon
(BCE), composed of representatves from several ministries, coordinates education, training, and
employment policies. Ihe structure, organization, and stadstical profile of the education system
are presented in Annexes 1. 2. and 2-1.

D. Sector Development Issues
1.  MM=
2.7   The ETE roundtable and the work conducted during project preparation Identified the
following aspects as the main deficiencies in primary education: (a) severe financial constraints; (b)
low and inequitable access; (c) poor quality of education; and (d) weak management and
supervision.
(a)    Severe finandial constraints
2.8   Following the war conditions from 1979-1983, public and private school attendance rose
rapidly. From 1984 to 1990, primay school enrollment increased at a rate of 5.4% per annum,
and the enrollment ratio increased from 51% to 59%.
2.9    Currently, government financing for education is 17%  of government reacu
expenditures. The budgetary allocation in Chad among primary, secondary and university
education has been well-balanced compared to most other countries in the region. The sha  of
prmary education in the education budget-above 64% between 1985 and 1987 whie the pimary
education system was being rebuilt-was 51% in 1991, compared to the SSA average of 42%. Tho
rent share of the education budget for post-primary levels are: gener  secondary (22%)
technical and vocational (2.5%), higher education (15%), primary and secondary teacher traing
(8%) and administration (1.5%).
2.10  In 1990-91, it is estimated that local communities-through their APE-conributed CFAP
317 millon (about 7% of the pmary recwurrent budget) toward primary education. Acording to a
recent survey, 71% of primary schools receive some financial support from APE. Community
efforts have included employing and maintaining 3900 'supplementary' teachers (40% of pimary
teahers nationwide), building and maintaining classrooms, and providing supplies, materals, and
school management. Community teachers receive-in cash and in kind-on average 5000 CFAF per
month. Some of these teachers-classified as bdnivole d'at-receive an additional 4000 CFAF
from the Government. Because of the low level and disparity of teacher salaries, the unit cost
(primay student/year) is only $23, compared to $69 for Francophone African countries. This
ranges from $6 in dcoks spontandes to $36 in schools which are fully staffed by civil servants.
2.11   The regular payment of all civil servants is a serious issue in Chad: currently, paymen  of
teachers' salaries are up to 6 months lae, particularly in ttie provinces.  The Government's
inability to deal with the problem of salry arrears has decreased both the caroom presence and
the level of motivation of civil service teachers, thereby severely lowering education quality.
These arrears are the result of two elements: cumbersome payment procedurs for all civil servants
and cbronic lack of fnancial liquidity. The salary payment system Is segmented: three regional
and ten deparmental treasuries in the prfectaes pay local civi sevns after receiving a cash
voucher from N'djamna, either with funds from locally-generated revenues or with fndS
delivered from the central truy. PrEfectwres with a deficit balance can receive support from
ether neighboring prefectoral treasuries or from the central treasury (Amex 2-2. Table lO. Ihe
montly establishment of payroll lists and the delayed delivery of the cash voucher cause a lengthy
payment ystem, even in the case of financial liquidity. When the national tesu  is not liquid,
payments are further delayed, particularly in prEfectres that are in permanent deficit (atha, BET,
Biltine, Guera, Mayo-Kebbi, Ouaddai, and Salamat). Approximately one-third of all primary



-4-
teachers are posted in these pr4fectures. From early 1991 tD January 1992, an experimental
scheme under the Fourth Educaion Project-monitored by the MNitries of Fince and Educaio-
-initiadly reduced payment delas. Bascally, this procedure consisted of simplifying the reWuar
verification of payroll lists and incresing the number of cash payers in N'djamdna nd In three
pr*Wctures. Due to liquidity problems, the elimination within the MEN of the executing directoae
(DAF), and the testing of a new payment procedure to identify 'ghost' civil servant, the scheme
was abandoned In February 1992 and has not been resumed.
2.12 Ihe fragility of this financial situation threatens educational standards at all levels as well
as tixe sustnOablity of community initiatives. The Government should not try to integrate all the
currently employed supplementary teachers and bdnOwles dWatot into the civil service: In 1991/92,
this would have increased the primary education budget by 33% without increasing the enrollment
rate. On the contrary, in order to reach the 65% overall enrollment target in 1998, to improve the
nationwide quality of primary education, and to sustain the development of primary education, the
proposed project assumes that (in addition to the teachers the Government will recruit and finance),
communities continue to support recurrent cost financing of primary education and at least provide
for 60 new teachers a year.
2.13 Despite significant community efforts in education, community schools are weak In
manaement of their own rources and in organization: parents would like support for
iprovement of the operation of their community schools in order to provide adequate and
effective primary education. In addition, the conventional private education sector In Chad is not
developed. The lack of incentives-chiefly because of excessive regulation as well as the general
climate of uncerainty-has hindered the development of traditional prvae sdcools.  ohe
Governmt is committed to promoting measures to support community and private schools (e.g.,
tax exemptions, increased access to sites, promotion of non religious subjects in Arabic schools).
(b)   Low and J     tequlebl access.
2.14 The primary school enrollment ratio in Chad is 59%4, compared to the Sahel average of
52% and the SSA average of 78%. The share of total primary enrollment constituted by girls Is
31%, the lowest in SSA. The average pupil/teacher ratio (67:1) is one of the highest In the region
(38:1 on average)5.
2.15  Across the counry, there are wide differences in enrollment ratios, ranging from 10% in
parts of the North to 115% in parts of the South (see Annexm2.). School enrollment is lowest in
fou (of six) northernprEfectures with 23% of the school-age population: Bildne (10.0% overall,
7.3% % for girl), Batha (12.7%, 3.7%) Ouaddai (14.9%, 5.8%) and Kanem (12.0%, 18.4%). In
these and other remote areas, schools are more expensive to build and serve less students per class:
the average class size in the ra north is about 20 students, compared to 35 in the nura south.
The large catchment radius in rural areas means long waing distc  and special food-supply
problems. A nationwide feeding program supported by the World Food Program (WFP) Is foud
in most rual schools (para. 3.54).

(c)    Po Quaty
2.19  Low rates of interna efficiency and examination pass rates nmifest the low quality of
primary education. Chad has one of the highest levels of repetition in primary education in the
world. The repetiion rate ranges from 30% to 36% between grades 1 and S and reaches 40% In
grade 6. Ultimately, only two students out of three pass from one grade to the next All told,
only 30% of the Inial cohort entering primary education reach grade 6, and only 22% evetualy
pass the fa examination (CEPE) to certi completion of primary education. Taking into account
repetition and dropout, two out of every three student years is wasted. This abysmal level of
internal efficiency is atttable to the poor learning conditions, poor teahing cadre, Insufficient
and poor educational supervision and monitoring, and deficiencies in the curriculum.
2.20  Pupils in Chad do not bave access to the minimum reading materi quhed for  fective
leaning.  The allocation of Government resources towards instuctional materials-less than
US$.50 per pupil per year excluding interaonal aid-is insufficien. By June 1993, the ogoing
education reiabiliation project wil have distrbuted 798,000 textbooks (522,000 under the IMA
credi and 276,000 financed by the French Cooperion) in  i subjects: French, reading,
mathmatics, Arabic, geogaphy, social sciences. Given the rapid increase of primay enrollment
and the Goverment arget of 65% of enrollment ratio by 1998, an increased investment In
textbooks is needed.

2.23  Pte-servlce training has not adequately prepaed teachers for real classroom sitations.
Too much emphasis has been put on general education at the expense of continuous skill
upgrading, the more effective training to Improve teaching skills. In order to reduce the share of
training programs devoted to general education, the Government began in 1991 to recruit more
among students at the end of the upper secondary cycle to become bhsteurs bachellers: the
current program includes 54%  bnsteurs bacheilers and 46%  ntfuewrs adjoims.  While
founded on good intentions, this decision proved costly: the average salary of an buntier
bachler is 25 %  more than that of an instuteur adjoim, but there is no evidence of a
corresponding improvement in pupils' leaning. To limit salary costs, the Government now plans
to recruit and train four   ftewrs adjois for every lnaw bachdier.
2.24 Ihe primary education curriculum is ill-adapted to the basic pedagogic requirements. The
content is too theoretical: there is not enough focus on basic liteacy and numeracy skills, nor on
up-to-date issues such as health, water sanitation, and protection of the enmronment. In addition,
the curriculum is based on passive teaching methods: primay education would be improved
through the use of more inductive and problem-solving methods that have relevance for pupils in
their spcific context. The Government has proposed to revise the primary curriculum (including
environmental, nutritional and health-related topics) over the next two years with support from
FAC.
(d)   Weak management and supervison
2.25  The coverage of the inspetorate system needs improvement. Each of the 34 primary
school Inspectors is expected to supervise-with pedagogical advisor  asistance-about 210
teachers. This high number combined with the large distances covered by each inspector and the
lack of transportation lead to very infrequent school visits. The lack of adequate supervision-
along with frequent changes of teachers' posts-generates a feeling of helplessness and
abandonment among teachers, especially the young and inexperienced.
2.26  Ihe dfectiveness of the inspectorate system also needs improvement. Tne inspectorate
staff do not bave up-o-date infmaton on general assistance to teachers and on monitorin of the
education system at the local level. This is compounded by their lack of decision-making
authority. he system would beneflt if inspectors and headmasters-acting according to standard
regulations-bad more responsibilities in managing schools at local levels. The Goverment plans
to expeiment with a decentralized approach-including the establishment of 14 DdMgadons
Prfeciorales de l'Edcadon (DP)-which would empower the inspectorates and the APE to make
decisions at local levels.
2.27  Prior to grade 6, monioring of student learning is the respnsibility of individual schools

teaching materias also suggest that student assessment between grades 1 and S may be ineffective;
many teachers and inspectors have limited test-designing sks and no access to teaching materials
which could help them improve. Ihe Government has proposed the Introduction of a pilot

2.    Genral SecWnd    Education
2.28  In the four years of lgXqUWndar education, repetition rates range from 23% to 35%
ard the nunber of pupil-years requested to produce one graduate from the four year cycle is 6.8.
Girls represent 17% of enrollment. In public schools, 50% of all candidates pass the BEPC, while
the candidates from privat schools have a 65% success rae. In uoper secondary educO=on.
repetidon rates range from 30% to 37%, and one out of two students leaves the system witfout a
baccalaurid. Girls represent only 10% of enrollment.
2.29 The overall quality of secondary education is adversely affected by a shortage of qualified
teachers. In 1990-91, it is esdmated that only 50% of secondary teachers were qualified to teach at
that level. Passive teaching methods-as well as the theoretical bias of the curriculum-are ill-
adapted to quality education, particularly in the scientfic streams. To cope with the large number
of unqualified teachers and improve quality, the Goverment plans to emphasize in-service training
at the secondary level as well as to revise the secondary curriculum with assistance from FAC.
3.   Maw             n
(a)   University Educaton
2.30  Financial and administrative management of the university is weak, particuarly i intr-
department coordinaion. The staff do not provide sufficient professional support and follow-up
for teachers and students: My lack technical expeience and efecdve management and budgeting
procedures. In addition, the infonnaion system is weak: education statistics are delayed becuse
of outof-date computer equipment. Ihe Government intends to coninue to streamline and
improve the administration of the University and to better manage the efficiency measures that
have already been introduced. The French Cooperation is providing technical assistamce,
equipment, logistical support and training of staff in order to improve overall University
management.
2.31   Despite weak managerial capacity, though the application of strict admission and
scholaship policies, the Government has managed to avoid the crisis in higher education now
faced by most Sahelian countries. First, enrollment at the University of Chad is not as crowded as
in other universities In the region: since 1987, the total number of students has remained below
3,000. The number of new enras deceased from 1,286 in 1988 to 1,278 in 1991: temporary
figures show only 1,080 new ents in 1992. The Govemment has now introduced even stronger
admission and scholarship reguations in order to avoid distortions. Baccalaureate graduates must
pass a University admissions test which is scaled according to the number of places avaiale;
quotas for each field have been established and applied since 1990. Second, the amount of
resources spent on student subsidies is moderate in the Sahellan context. No first year university
studets receive scholarships, and they are awarded on the basis of merit starting the second year.
The monthly scholarship amount (25,000 CFAF) has remain unchanged since 1972. Total higher



-8-

compared to 21% on aveage for the SaheI6.
(b)   Weak Manageial and Plannng Capacities
2.32  The Mnistry of Education (MEN) includes 12 National and 14 Regional Dkectoate.
Their responsibllities are not clearly defined, and roles and functions tend to overlap.
Adminisrton remains highly centralized: even routine decisions-such as the dhanging of
teacheras' posts-a  made in N'djamdna  Internal communication and coordination among the
Ministy and the regional units are ineffective, with limited sharing of experiences. Additionally,
the staff consits largely of teachers untrained in strategic thinking, policy analysis, investment
planning, budget management, or personnel management techniques. To remedy these problems,
MEN intends to decentralize educational planning and resource management, to facilitate local
Iniiaves, and to provide inspectors with adequate budgetary and management authority to support
teachers and work with local communities and NGOs. In addition, MEN plans to develop
managerial and plnnig capacities within the Ministry, emphasize budget preparation and control,
and improve personnel mnagement techniques and donor coordination.
e. MTe Goveunment's Srategy
2.33   he goal of the Govemment's Education-Training-Employment roundtable in November,
1990 was to develop a sound sectoral strategy and to rebuild the education system without
repeating the costly errors of neighboring countries (e.g., over-ension of university
scholasp, use of wastefl school construction techniques, over-reiance on public sector
financng). Chadians from many backgrounds and sectors (maniny from the public sphere) and
most major donors attended. The resulting policy document-adopted by the Government in
December, 1990-presents various optons for education and taining and examines a range of
chall   , inluding macro, budgetary and employment consaints.   Subsequently, the
Govement established a technical unit (CIS) to coordinate the education work program and to
develop a sub-progrm by levels and types of education. each sub-program was reviewed withn
the Govenment and discussed with the donors. The CIS has performed its role well and has
provided a critical link between the MEN and the donors in education policy analysis and project
developmenL
2.34  For prmary education, the key elements retained by the ETE sategy (and supported by
the proposed project) are: (a) to expand access to pry education by allocatng more public
resources for the education sector, lncluding increasing the  nber of primary teachers; (b) to
ensure a more Intensive utilizadon of teachers and facilities by augmenting the use of double-shif
and multigrade teaching tecbiques; (c) to improve peformance and acquiiion of basic skills by
prmting eher retg, textbook distribution, and more pedagogic support; and (d) to
promoto privat and communiy intatives by developing a partnerhip with APE and NGOs at
local levels. For seconday and higher educadon, the Government plan to continue Ot apply
admssion reglaions. For inst nal development, the Government plans to stengthen the
analytdal and mmaamet capacity of central and decentrlized uns in planning, budgeting,
aluato, and monitoring.

F. The Bank Group's Role
2.35  Lssons learned from previous projects. This would be the fifth IDA-fianced educadon
project in Chad. From 1968 until the war period, IDA financed three projects. Ihe first two-Cr.
126-CD, US$2.2 million, signed in 1968; Credit 251-CD, US$2.7 million, 1971-were completed
in eight years, four years later than planned. Th third-Cr. 811-CD, US$8.3 million, 1978-was
completed in 6 years, following an interrupdon during the civil war. These projects supported
investments in teacher-training and technical centers, primary education iastructure, disition
of educational materials, and management and planning of the education sector. The Project
Completion Reports of these ree projects-all rated unsaisfactory by OED-offer the following
lessons: (a) the projects were over-ambitious, with components in agricultural, techical and
general education; (b) the Government had difficulty in assming recurrent costs, for which no
specific finacing mechanism was developed; (c) project institutions were inadequately staffed; (d)
mainteance of physical components was sustained only when school directors and users were held
acocountable; (e) IDA supervision, which relied largely on cownsut , was insufficient; and (fM the
donors-particlarly bse of lack of coordination-were not able to provide adequate  ical
assistance as the project developed.
2.36  Implemention of the ongoing Education Rehabilitation Project (Cr. 19504C, US$25.2
million, 1988)-which has supported sectoral rehabilitation-is proceeding steadily after a difficult
two-year start-up (including looting and damaging of the MEN building and 30 project vehicles
during the December, 1990 political events). Performance has been good in all pedagogic aspects
and in the development of a long-term sectoral strategy. Disbursement is on schedule, and most
covenants are in compliance: the project is attaining its development objective of rehabiitating
primary education and the sector's management/planning capacities while laying the grundwork
for future development."7 This generally positive picture has been marred by two Issues. First,
the constuction componen was delayed due to a long bureaucratic bidding process and to an over-
estimation of the capacity of one NGO to oversee construction of 1,000 classes. Second, the
Government has failed to address the question of teacher salary arreas (ara. 2.11).
2.37  Rtiale for IDA involvement. IDA's coun  strategy for Chad supports acdvities that:
(a) address basic welfare issues; (b) place minimal demands on govemen resources; (c) make
maximum use of non-Government capabilites through reliance on NGOs, local communities and
the private sector as implemenng agents; and (d) improve the prowpects for long-term growth.
The proposed project is a central part of this assistance program. It would also form part of IDA's
long-tem education strategy in Chad-including possible operations in vocational training and in
higher education. As a key partner in the development of the ETE strategy and in the
establishment of macroeconomic policy, IDA can continue to effectively promote a susinable
education policy during the project period.

exclusively for teachers in order to speed up and secure the internal processing of their salaries

witbin the master computeized system for civil servant salary payment. The TOP would be

bank. In order to ensure its sustainability, the balance would be equivalent to approximately three
months salaries for all primary teachers in prectures with a chronic deficit (CFAF 300 million)".

B. ProWect Cost and Tindng Plan
3.52   The total project cost over the five years of implementation is estmated, net of taxes, at
US$33.8 million, with a base cost of US$29.7 million and a foreign exchange component of
US$17.4 million. Price contingencies, estimated at US$2.6 million, assume an annual domestic
inflation rate of 3% and a foreign inflation rate of 3.7%; physical confingencies are estimated at
US$1.4 million (4.8% of base costs). The project would be fianced by IDA (US$19.3 million,
57% of total costs), KfW (US$3.4 million, 10.2%), GTZ (US$2.2 million, 6.5%), contrutions
by local communities to primary school construction, teachers' salaries, and local pedagogic
improvements projects (JS$0.9 milion, 3.0%); and budgetary allocations from the Government
(JS$7.9 million, 23%). KfW would cofinance the constuction of primary classrooms; GmZ
would assist in the monitoring, and coordination of the local pilot projects and in the main ce
of the objective-oriented planning approach of the project. The major assumptions in ariving at
the cost estimates and the detailed project costs-including by component, cost category and source
of financing-are given in Annex 1.

IV PROJECT  IPLEMETATION
A. Status of Project Pfraradion and Readines
4.1 To assure local ownership and to improve the quality of entry, project preparation was
conducted at the regional and national levels using a participatory approach. First, the IDA
preparaon mission and staff of the MEN initiated and attended two regional stakeholder
conferences in Sahr and Bongor: inspectors, directors, and teachers; members of local APE and
NGOs; and representatives of women's groups evaluated local problems in primary education and
presented possible solutions. Subsequently, the MEN initiated two similar regional conferences in
Abeche and Moundou as well as a five-day seminar in N'djamena on decentralization of the
education system. In a final six-day conference during the pre-appraisal mission, the logical
framework (cadre logque) of the project (A&ex 3) was constructed using the ZOPP methodology,
a participatory planning tool developed by G0Z (provided as an input by Germy) desiged to
clarify project objectives and activities among critical stakeholders. Participants in the conference
included staff of the MEN, representatves from the regional stakeholder conferences, staff of
NGOs, and members of the IDA mission. Additional preparation activities-including pre-
investment studies-were coordinated by the current PCU and MEN teams, assisted by other
donors (UNICEF, UNDP, FAC) and consultants financed under the ongoing project During the
prais  mission, represenatves of the Getman Government-including BMZ, G1Z, and KfW-
contributed to project development. Throughout project preparation, dissions were regularly
held with other donors-particularly with FAC, 017, KfW, UNDP, UNICEF, and EEC-to ag
on priorities within the ETE strategy. Working papers for all projects components have been
prepared. Architecurl designs for primary school constuction and a procurement schedule are
avable; the remaining documentation for civil works, including draft bid docments have been
reviewed during negotiations.

(August 1991). Signature of a contract with a CMA for school construction management would be
a condidon gf credkeffetiveness. Civil works contracts would be awarded based on procedures
described in an imple  ion manual (similar to that introduced for Senegal) as agreed with
Government during negotiations. The CMA would maintain a roster of pre-qualified contractors
eligible for Local Competitive Bidding (LCB), would permit additional applications at any time,
and would not preclude large or foreign firms from participating. Standard bidding documents
would propose a reference price and indicate unit prices and quantities. Conactors would be
Invited to bid by way of discounts either on the total or on the unit prices. Contacts would be
awarded to the lowest evaluated bid based on criteria specified in bidding documents. Ihe contract
award committee of CMA would open bids in public and have full authority to contract. It would
justify, however, in writing and prior to signing the contract and with reference to the bidding
documents' criteria, any decision not to select the lowest evaluated bid. For contracts with fewer
than five bidders, or cons greater than or equal to US$200,000 equivalent, the award
committee would request IDA approval prior to sigilng the contract. The CMA would contract
supervision of construction to firms and NGOs (for remote areas) selected in accordance with The

4.13 Reie by IDA. During project supervision, IA-financed contracts for works and goods

4.14  The project is expected to be completed over the five-year period; the IDA credit would be
disbursed over 6 years (aceording to the categories shown in Table 3 below; Chad's standard
disbursement profile is seven years). Disbursement of the Credit would be fully documented
except for contracts and expenditures valued less than US$20,000 equivalent which would be made
against Statements of Expenditure (SOEs). Documentation for withdrawals under SOEs would be
retained at the PCU for review by IDA supervision missions and for semiannual audits. To
facilitate disbursement, the Government would open a SpecW Account (SA) in a commercial bank
to cover WDA's share of eligible expenditures managed by the PCU. The authorized allocation for
the Special Account would be CFAF 150 million. WA would make an initial deposit of that
amount from the proposed Credit upon credit effectiveness and would replenish the SA upon
receipt of satisfactory proof of incurred eligible expenditures. Replenishment requests would be
accompanied by up-to-date bank statements and reconciliations of the SA. Applications for direct
paymens and reimbursement or requests for special commitments would apply to contracts above
US$20,000 equivalent.
F. Accounting, Auditing and Reporting
4.15   The PCU would be responsible for the project financial management and would maintin
consolidated project accounts following a system satisfactory to WA. Project accounts, including
the SA, would be audited annually by independent auditorsW acceptable to IDA; all disbursements
under SOEs would be audited semdanuaily, and the special financing mechanism for teachers
salaries would be audited quarterly. The annual audit report would be submitted to IDA within six
months of the end of each fiscal year; the semiannual audit reports would be submitted to IDA
within three months of each period; the quarterly audit reports of the special financing mechanism
woud be transmitted within forty five days following each quarter. The Government has submitted
a short-list of independent auditors, including the related draft contract and terms of reference.
Ihe Government gave assurances during negotiations that It would submit to IDA audit reports of
reasonable scope and details within: (i) six months of the end of each fiscal year in the case of the
annual audit report; (ii) three months of the end of each semester in the case of the SOEs; and (iii)

V.    PROJECT BENEFITS AND RISKS
5.1   pzo    Benft. Implementation of the overall education program supported by dlis
project would provide additional enrollment capacity for approximately 117,000 additional pupils
from 1994 to 1998. Ihe main direct impact of the project would be to provide classrooms for
55,000 pupils, largely in rural areas, where 84% of project financed classrooms would be
constructed. It is expected that the primary enrollment ratio in rural areas would increase from
S1% to 59%, whereas that of urban areas would improve from 91% to 95%. Since repetition is
expected to decline from 30% to 20%, intake rates into grade one of primary education would
increase more rapidly (from 64% in 1990 to 75% in 1998), and the number of school years
invested for each student who reaches grade 6 would decline from 18 to 9 by the eand of the
project. In addition, project investments in textbooks, teacher training, girls' education as well as
project support for secoral policies would improve education quality and efficiency throughout the
sector. Project qualitative Inputs and policy measures to limit repetition would improve quality
throughout the primary education system, thereby lowering the average number of years it takes to
graduate. In addition, pilot school improvement projects would further improve education quality



32-
and strghen local idatives In thre regions coverng 36% of pimary school rollm    in
1990. Support for ins"tional developmen would ipro  managenmet and sustainability of the
education sector, and budgetary policy measures wodd prevent distortions In resource allocation
within the education sector. In the long-term, the prjec would ras the productvity of the labor
force and contibute to Inceased well-being among the Chadian population.
5.2   RlWa. lhe file political envitoment and weak implement  capacity could Jeopardize
project imple n . To  _"min   the effect of tis risk, the project woud promote the
Govemet's commitme  to: (a) decentralize educaton managemet; (b) reinforc the opertnal
responsibility and magement of schools by regional direrates; (c) rely on the private sector,
NMOs, and communies to manage construction and school imptovement projects; and (d) further
develop sakeholder commitment through the contued use of participatory, objectve-iented
project plang and implemeion methods (ZOPP). Ihe support for local school mprowvement
Projects is lso designed to build on the reslience ad ecuting capcity of loed communities.
Another risk i8 that the Government may fce difficulties In regulary paying teachers salaries.
Ihis risk would be addressed dlrecty by the introduction of th special payment mechanism to
facilitate regular salary payment for primary school teachers. In addition, this risk would be
midgated by (a) consesus-building with the Government under the EiT   famework; (b)
priorizing this issue in macroeconomic dialogue among the IDP, IlDA and the Goeen  (c)
maintaining close codon with and support from the Prenich and other bilateral agencies; (d)
monitoring of public expendiures for primary education through the special payment mechanism
and during annual reviews of project implemenaion; and (e) mantaing high levels of community
financing.

BASIC EDUCATION PROJECT (EDUCATION V)
ANNUAL MONITORING INDICATORS AND LOGICAL FRAMEWORK
Annual monitoring indicators (p. 2) bave been developed in order to assess progms in the
implemenion of the primay education section of the ETE strategy and In related projec
activities. These indicators are based upon the Logical Framework (Cadre Loglqu) (pp. 3-12),
which was developed In a participatory workshop durlng project pre-appralsal, folowing three
regional stakeholder conferences.
The Logical Framework includes the objectives and outputs (part 1) and actives (part 2) of the
proposed proJect.
Part 1 (pp. 348) includes the following column:
I.             d         A summary of the genal objectives of the ptojec and of th
outpts of each component and sub-c   e;
2.        b:      Measum  (direct or nirect) to verily to what extet th objectves and
outputs have been achieved;
3.      sagua hor   of data neceary to verify the stas of the objective and
outt Inicats;
4.                         . Important eve, conditions, or decisions which must
prevail in order to aeve the read objective or output
Part 2 (pp. 9-12) includes the following columns:
1.    Ress:  The activities to be undertake  by th  project to support the related
outputs;
2.      Tas: The goods ad seces necsay to undertake the activities, including unit
costs;
3.     p:f.  The total cos of each componen snd sub-component;
4.    A        The skeholders who wlll be involved in each activity.

REPUBL1C OF CHAD
BASIC EDUCATION PROJECT (EDUCATION V)
LErM2R OF EDUCATIONl DEVELOPMENT POLICYI
This letter of Education Policy confirms the Government of Chad's intendons to put
in place an education project in order to realize the development strategy defined in the sector. This
project subscribes to both the Education-Training-Employment (ETE) strategy defied by the
Government and the direct continuation of the Education Rehabilitation project. It aims at revitalizing
the sector and engaging it on a sustainable development path for the improvement of the economic
and social retns on the investments in the sector, notably those foreseen in the scope of the Basic
Education project (Education V). This project will be an investment project which will include a set
of policy measures. The objective of these policy measures adopted by the Government will aim at:
(a)   increasing public expenditures on primary education;
(b)   applying efficiency measures in primary education;
(c)   promoting girls' participation in primary education;
(d)   promoting the expansion of private and community initiatives in education.
One of the most serious problems with which Chad is confronted with regard to
economic and social growth concerns the low level of human resource development, particularly the
low rate of literacy and the drop in the education quality Indicators. Ihis situation requires that our
efforts be concentrated on the essential, and that the problems be tackled in a now manner through
policy measures concerning the management of the education system. The Government places
priority on the resolution of these problems in the scope of its sectoral Education-Training-
Employment strategy, and in its general strategy for the future of the country. Primary Education
constitutes the priority in the Government's educational policy in order to lay the foundation for the
country's economic development and improve the productivity of the labor force, the sanitary and
nutritional state of the population, particularly the vulnerable groups-women and children. Actions
will also be undertaken to reinforce girls' education. With this in mind, a 65% primary education
enrollment rate by 1998 is indispensable.
Measures alraytake
In the scope of the Education Rehabilitation Project. the Government implemented a certin
number of measures aimed at increasing the public resources allocated to the education sector, and
improving their efficient use. As a result, education financing by the Government, which represented
12% of the Government expenditures budget in 1988 increased to 17% in 1991. During the same
time period the primary education's share of the education operating budget reached 51%.
Experimentation with double-shift teaching was put in place in 19 classes to dtermine the operating
conditions by which this organization can be expanded to a substantial mmiber of urban schools.
Multigrade classes were also expanded in the rural zones and in 1991-92 represented 35% of the rural
classes. Community initiatives in the creation of schools were not slowed. Local communities have
employed and maintained 40% of primary teachers. In addition, measures were taken to improve
1.    This is an unofficial translation of the original French document.



- 64 -
ANNEX4
Page 7 of 8
the management of the education system, notably by the creation of Prefctural Delegtio  for
Education with a view to better coordination of educational performance at the regional levd, and
to reorce responsibilities of the decentralized units.
In order to contain higher education costs the Government introduced admission tests which
are scaled according to the number of places available. To adjust the number of students by fields,
a student orientation policy towards scientific and technical instuction was put in place. In addition,
the scholarships are not given until the second year of university studies. This policy allowed the
volume of scholarships to be maintained at a reasonable level.
Measures taken for the period 1994-1998. With a view to improving resource use in the
sector, and to qualitatively and quantitatively develop primary education, Chad wiUl implement the
following measures:
Increase the available resources to develop primary education and im=rove its qaity. In
addition to the decisions taken to improve resource use, the Governme  will make sufficient
resources available to primay education to ensure its development:
(a)    a construction program for 1,700 classrooms wiUl be completed between 1994 and 1998, an
average of 340 each year. The construction program will be delegated to a contract
management agency (AGETIPE). NGOs, Parents' Associations (APE) and communities
participation partnerships will be reinforced with a view to increasing their capacities,
reducing costs and improving construction quality;
(b)   the material and primary school supplies budget for primaq education wfll be progressively
Increased to reach 200 milion FCFA in 1998;
(c)    on average, at least 380 new teachers wiUl be trained annually in the teacher training colleges,
and 280 new teaching posts wfll be added annually to the Government's primary education
budget;
(d)    a special mechanism will be put in place to assure the regular payment of the primary
teachers' salaries.
Increase the efficiency in the use of primary teachers and classrooms.
(a)    Utilization of the classrooms and primary teachers. It has been observed in Chad that in a
situation in which a teacher is in charge of a small number of students (which is most often
the case in the rural zones with their dispersed settlements), or in a situation in which the
classrooms are overcrowded, it is still not possible to satisfy all the enrollment demands. For
the first situation, multigrade classes continue to be applied as often as possible. For the
second, the composition of the double-shift classes (one classroom for a maitre and two
groups of students) will be utilized progressively in the urban zones to reach 1,200 cohorts
of 55 students eac by 1998. Ihese two measures are of a nature to lead to substatial
economies which will aid in the development of primary education.
(b)    Reduce the repetition rate in primary classes. The Government will abolish, as of October
1994, repetition in gades 1, 3 and 5; and will reasonably reduce the level of repetition in



- 65 -
ANNEX A
Page 8 of 8
grades 2, 4 and 6. The stdents will be taught by the same teacher throughout the same
cycle (1-2; 3-4; and 5-6).
(c)    Reform primary education teacher training. It has been established in the pre-service teacher
traning system that the programs, methodological steps implemented, and the plans used
should be reviewed to assure better preparation by the teachers for real classroom situations.
With regard to the pre-service taining, it was agreed to introduce a reform having the goal
of correcting the theoretical predominance and concentrating on the practical aspects, notably
in diversifying the field training in the schools and increasing their duration. Concerning in-
service training, it is necessary to reinforce the actions of decentralized training, conducted
near the teachers' work environment which is always more efficient, and to this end improve
the training of the trainers and increase their means of action. Within etain limits, the
competence of a primary school teacher depends more on the quality of the training received
ta on the academic level at recruitment. As a consequence the recruitment of fiture
teachers will take place in all the ENI based on 80% futdtewes adjoins and 20% iftweurs
bacdiers.
AdQoin of provisions aimed at increasing girls' education:
(a)    girls' education will be reinforced by appropriate measures notably in the rural zones where
inequality of access to schools poses problems;
(b)   realization of a study proposing realistic solutions to be Implemented to improve and
reinforce gids' participation in education at all levels of the education system;
(c)    creation of a unit attached to the Minister's office and charged with promotion of girls'
education.
(d)   inoduction in the prservice and in-service teacher trauiing programs of modules of
information and sensitivity training on the Importance of girls' education; and
(e)    a national sensitivity campaign on the importance of girls' education.
Eage Ura tiatives in the grovision of education. In the scope of Chad's general
strategy for the development of human resources, and considering the budgetary constraints which
weigh on the sector, studies will be undertaken with a view to adopting the measures to help the
development of the private sector. Local communities, particularly the APE, play a determning role
in the fuctioning of the educational system in Chad. The Government believes that this effort, which
is indipnsable to the efficiency of the education system, deserves supports. In this scope, based on
criteria which will be determined by the MEN, community schools (called spontaneous schools) could
benefit from canteens financed by the PAM.
The Govermment is convinced that the whole of these measures combined with investments
envisaged in the scope of the education development project, wiUl help the Chadian educatlon system
develop more efficiency, more equality and a relevancy to the social and economic development of
the country.

REPUBTIQUE OF CHAD
BASIC EDUCATION PROJECT (EDUCATION V)
APPUI AUX ASSOCIATIONS DE PARENTS D'ELEVES
A. Cadre g6ndral et diagnostic
1I.1 Au Tchad, pratiquement chaque ecole dlementaire et mOme secondaire a une APE. Les activites
des APE sont souvent l'affaire des seuls parents d'eleves. Tous les parents des anciens et nouveaux
6lbves inscrits dans une ecole sont par definition membres de l'APE de cette dcole et doivent I ce titre
remplir certaines obligations: (i) Paiement (quasi) obligatoire de la cotisation de l'APE fixee par
enfant scolarise et pouvant varier d'une annee I l'autre en fonction des besoins; (ii) Participation aux
reunions de l'APE.; (iii) Participation a la demande du comite de l'APE, aux activitEs de l'association;
(ii) Assistance dans l'encadrement des eleves; etc.. Le recouvrement des cotisations (taux de 40 a
60%), n'a &te jusqu'ici possible qu'a cause de son caractere "obligatoire."
1.2 En milieu rural, 1'esprit communautaire est encore relativement fort. Les infrastructures du
village appartiennent I tous les villageois. L'ecole, le poste de santd, le magasin villageois, la
pharmacie, I'hydro-pompe etc sont g6rds par la communautE. En milieu urbain, le fonctionnement
des APE rencontrent plus de difficultes. La vie sociale y est plus individualisee, les conflits d'indr*t,
les contraintes socio-economiques et les charges sociales plus marquees et plus lourdes. De manibro
gnErale il y a un probleme de ddpendance des APE vis-a-vis des direteurs d'ecoles. Les dirct
d'dcoles jouent un rOle dterminant dans le fonctionnement des APE. En fait, Us sont les pilers des
APE. Chacue annee, la quasi-totalite des APE se reconstitue grace I l'initiative des directeurs
d'ecoles. C'est encore I l'initiative de ces der*iers que la plupart des rEunions du comite et les
Assemblees GEndrales ont lieu.
B. Objectif de la composante
1.3 L'objectif de cette composante est d'appuyer les initiatives des APE en les aidant I s'auto-
organiser, I mieux gerer leurs ressources, et I developper des activitds Educatives Elargies qui vont
au doll des contributions financibres babituelles (construction de classes, achat de mobilier etc). 1l
s'agit par des actions de formation, et d'encadrement de: (i) assurer l'auto-dveloppement des APE;
(ii) de reduire leur dependance vis I vis des directeurs d'ecole; (iii) de favoriser des activites
zontinues des APE, et (iv) de mieux les preparer I travailler en collaboration avec les ONG et les
enseignants.  Dans ce cadre, i s'agira aussi d'integrer dans le programme de formation des
enseignants, un module sur 'I'Animation et la gestion des APE" afin de donner aux futurs
enseignants, les aptitudes necessaires pour soutenir valablement les APE. Le directeur d'ecole dans
le cadre de cetto strategie reste un conseiller de l'APE sans pouvoir dElibtratif, mais avec un rOle
important de formation et d'encadrement. L'objectif de son travail doit tre celui d'aider l'APE I
devenir capable de s'auto-organiwr.
C. Mise en oeuvre
1.4 Pour assurer un cadre lEgal favorable au developpement des APE, I'Administrion tchadiemne
est en train de finaliser un texte legal intitulE 'Ordonnance portant statut des AssociatIom des
Pafents d'Efves". L'objet de ce texte est de crder le cadre gEndral de rEglementation et de
fonctionnoment des APE sur le territoire du Tchad ainsi que leur relation avec l'Administration.
L'approche de cette composante est de rEpondre aux besoins identifies et exprimEs par les APE. Pour

REPUBLIC OF C
BASIC EDUCATION PROJECT (EDUCATION V)
PROJECT MONITORING AND SUPERVISION PLAN
1.1  IDA  suervision  input.   Regular supervision  needs are described  in Table 1 of this
Annex. In addition, specific tasks such as review of progress reports, terms of reference, annual plans and
audits, and pocurment and disbursement actions, are estimated to require 8 staff weeks of various specialist
inputs a year which are not included in Table I.
Bonower's Contribution to Supervision
(a)      Pmgts repots would be submitted twice a year, in October and April. They would include
a summnay of implementation under each project component. financial statements on projet
expenditues, updated project implementation (includmg the special mechanism for ktacher
salaries payment- SMTS-) and procurement schedules, as well as the status of conat
advertising, bidding, awards and completion dates and compliance with aggregate limits on
specified methods of procurement. The PIU would collect inputs from each responsible
MENES Directorate and prepare the reports.
(b)      Joint annual reviews. Once a year, no earlier than october and not later than November 30,
the PIU will organize a joint IDA/Government review of project implementation based on:
(i) the progress report in the above paragraph which, for the pumposes of the annual review,
wod also include: (ii) an annual work program and budget for the next academic year; (iii)
a draft budget for the education sector for the next year; (iv) the status of policy and project
monitoring indicators as listed in the ' cadre logique;"and (v) the status of SMTS
implementation. The annual reviews would be held at the national and regional levels using
the ZOPP approach developed during project preparation.
(c)      Mid-term review. In addition, the PIU would carry out, jointly with IDA, and, to the extent
available, with other donors affiliated with the project ( namely KfW and GTZ) and with
Cooperation Prsncaise, not earlier than April I and not later than May 31, 1996, a mid-term
review of the progress made in carrying out the project. The review would cover all aspects
of the project, with a special focus on: (i) progress in implementing policy measues and their
impact on the staffing, cost and efficiency of the system, including double-shifting in urbn
classrooms, lowering repetition in primary schools, the recruitment of teachers, and
completing the teacher in-service and pre-service traiing restucturing program; (ii) the
effectiveness of the different modalities of implementing the classroom construction
component and the ability of communities to finance their expected share; (iii) the
effectiveness of the SMTS; (iv) progress in implenenting the pilot 'school program'; (iv)
functioning of the girls' education programs; (v) progress m the studies on promoting private
school, and improving MEN management system; and (vi) progress in the implementation of
the programs financed by other donors, particularly KfW, GTZ, and the Cooperation
FranQaise. Based on the review, the MEN shall promptly prepare an action plan, acceptable
to IDA, for the further implementation of the project and the program, and shall thereafter
implement such action plan.
(d)      The PIU would be responsible for coordinating arrangements for IDA supervision missions
and participating in the mission with tnem.
(e)      Pffijemption         . Within six months of the Credit closing date, a completion report,
prepared in accordance with terms of reference satisfactory to IDA, would be utrnited to
IDA.
